FROM:  San Mateo Heritage Alliance 
SUBJECT: New development projects in and around Downtown 
Dear Ms. Sandhir, 
San Mateo Heritage Alliance would like to schedule a meeting with Planning and Community 
Development to discuss the following concerns regarding development projects in Downtown: 
1.
We have noticed that most projects proposed in and around Downtown appear to share similar 
design characteristics that diverge from established building patterns.  
2.
We have noticed that developers appear confused and conflicted by the design guidance and their 
muddled design proposals reflect that confusion and conflict. 
3.
We are concerned that the truly important issues of contextual responsibility and urban continuity 
are not being addressed.   
4.
We are concerned that absent traditional characteristics and consistent patterns that define urban 
commercial architecture, the intended compatibility with the historic Downtown core will not be 
achieved.  
5.
We ask that development projects proposed for the greater Downtown area be reviewed by qualified 
historic preservation architects for design compatibility and contextual continuity. 
6.
We ask that qualified historic preservation architects experienced in writing design guidelines for 
Downtowns and historic districts, be engaged to prepare new design guidelines for Downtown San 
Mateo.  
7.
We ask that planning staff consult Redwood City’s 2011 Downtown Plan which provides a good 
model for appropriate architectural character types and styles. 
8.
We are concerned that if the current process continues without intervention, the result will be - as 
the city’s Alternative Analysis warned - “development incompatible with the existing historic fabric 
surrounding the Downtown Historic District.”  It is an outcome no one wants. 
www.smheritage.org

Downtown possesses a diversity of building types and architectural styles spanning over a hundred 
years.  Yet the myriad of buildings proposed for Downtown lack the necessary style references to the 
rich architectural context of the Downtown environment.  All share a disconcertingly similar appearance 
that challenges the very meaning of compatibility with the established Downtown character, predominant 
building typology, and architectural pattern of development.   
San Mateo Heritage Alliance remains committed to working with the City of San Mateo to ensure and 
encourage new development that is compatible and complimentary to the Downtown Historic District.    
We would welcome the opportunity to meet soon, perhaps in the next couple of weeks, to discuss our 
concerns and offer our input and assistance in helping to craft regulations that meet contemporary 
needs while strengthening Downtown’s historic sense of place.

--- page 127 ---
SAN MATEO
HERITAGE ALLIANCE
June 6, 2023 
Appendix A 
Issues of Concern 
Compatibility and Typology 
Downtown San Mateo has a wide variety of architectural styles and building typologies that provide the 
setting and establish the context for new development.  The Downtown Specific Plan and Downtown 
Design Guidelines direct new development to be “compatible” with the existing building fabric. 
Main Streets and commercial Downtowns nationwide are all recognizable and familiar to us because 
they share a set of common typological characteristics and compositional vocabulary. They are formally 
consistent, stylistically diverse, and able to accommodate functional change. In his seminal book, The 
Buildings of Main Street: A Guide to American Commercial Architecture, noted architectural historian 
Richard Longstreth traces the evolution of the compositional vocabulary used in every American Main 
Street over the past two centuries and presents a method of identifying building characteristics 
commonly found in central commercial districts up to the mid-20th century. In so doing, Longstreth 
helps us understand how to make planning decisions shaped by historical perspective rather than 
current aesthetic preference. 
Architectural Style and Unified Design 
Successful and enduring buildings generally have one unified architectural style that integrates the entire 
building in one harmonious composition. The proposed projects, however, disregard this practice, giving 
the upper floors a completely different design appearance than the lower floors.  The effect is one of 
confused discord rather than unity and harmony.  
Redwood City has addressed the issue of creating architectural character in new projects compatible 
with the established architectural patterns in their Downtown by creating architectural “Character 
Zones.”  Within these zones, different “Character Types” - Neoclassical, Victorian, Craftsman, 
Mediterranean, Art Deco, and Contemporary are permitted.   
These Architectural Character Types are intended to reinforce both the predominant eclectic nature of 
building fabric in the Downtown area, and the strong preferences for an appropriate aesthetic for all new 
buildings and development.  SMHeritage believes this type of approach is appropriate and would like to 
encourage its use in Downtown San Mateo to help ensure that new development is compatible and 
complementary to the historic resources. 
Massing 
Massing, more than any architectural detail, has the most impact on the eye.  Consequently it is one of 
the most important design considerations.  The multiple masses and volumes of most of the proposed 
projects, however, read like an assortment of different size shipping boxes rather than unified 
architectural compositions. 
www.smheritage.org
Page  of 
3
8

--- page 128 ---
SAN MATEO
HERITAGE ALLIANCE
June 6, 2023 
Building Corners 
Generally, buildings in an urban context should maintain a consistent street wall along their street 
frontages.  Corner towers and recessed corner entries are widely used to emphasize the corner of a 
building at specific intersections and to capture pedestrian traffic.  But to eliminate the corner altogether, 
or replace it with a multi-story glass box as some of the projects do, does more to diminish the 
streetscape and urban experience than to enhance it.   
Daylight Plane 
Building heights in Downtown San Mateo vary from one story to twelve, and in the Historic District from 
one to nine stories.  Street wall frontages for virtually all existing buildings rise up to full height without 
setbacks. Yet, the daylight plane requirement for the entire retail core limits the height of the street 
frontage to one half the street width - generally two to three stories - regardless of the prevailing street 
wall pattern and varied building height allowance of 55 to 75 feet.  
This seems to be a one-size-fits-all requirement that could be 
adjustable and tailored to differing established building patterns in 
different parts of Downtown.   
One successful infill project within the Historic District is 101 
Ellsworth, rising six stories without an upper level setback, 
suggesting that upper floor setbacks may not be the only solution 
for new construction in and around the Historic District. 
Examples of Compatible Downtown Infill Projects 
Four examples of compatible new infill construction are recognizable in Downtown: Century Movie 
Theater, the Transit Center, the Main Street Parking Garage and 101 Ellsworth.  They achieve their 
compatibility primarily because they conform to established commercial building typologies and draw on 
recognizable architectural styles. Inspiration for the new work came from Mediterranean Revival and Art 
Deco styles popular during the 1890-1950 Historic District period of significance, yet the design of each 
building was interpreted in a contemporary way and meets contemporary needs.   
 
www.smheritage.org

Appendix B 
Proposed New Construction in a Historic Context 
What is the inspiration behind some of the new infill projects proposed for Downtown?  Do they conform 
to familiar commercial building typologies?  Do they reflect historical precedents and patterns of 
development?  Do they draw on known architectural styles familiar to and popular with the public?  Are 
they respectful to the surrounding community?  Are they worthy of being landmarks themselves in 
50-100 years?  Although there are at least a dozen or more projects that cause us concern, we think the 
eight examples discussed below are sufficient to convey our point.  
* * * 
Paste-on Parody 
Located just east of the railroad in the Downtown support area, Block 21 and 3rd & Claremont seem to 
find inspiration in a controversial preservation technique known as facadism.  Facadism is sometimes 
used to construct a new building behind a historic facade.  In successful examples, the new building is 
completely hidden behind the old facade.  That is not the case here.  
Neither design acknowledges the established  pattern of providing changes to the street fronting facade 
every 25 to 50 feet as advised in the Downtown Design Guidelines.  Neither reflects the essential 
typological or stylistic patterns prevalent in the greater Downtown.  The question is where did the 
developer get the idea these designs would be acceptable or welcomed by the surrounding community. 
www.smheritage.org
Page  of 
5
8
3rd & Claremont
Block 21 
Facadism
Facadism

--- page 130 ---
SAN MATEO
HERITAGE ALLIANCE
June 6, 2023 
Cornerless Brick Warehouses 
Three projects - 31-57 S. B St. (abutting the Historic District), 405 E. 4th Ave., and 222 E 4th Ave - seem 
to draw their inspiration from brick warehouses and factories that proliferated during the mid-19th 
century at the height of the industrial revolution.  Popular in the mid to late 1800s, they were generally 
constructed in the industrial areas of cities, not the Downtown commercial core of urban centers which 
featured retail shops, mercantile and business offices.   
Although sometimes brick cladding may be the appropriate material, and a few of the oldest Downtown 
buildings are brick, the use of brick does not necessarily equate to compatibility with the Historic District 
where concrete, stucco, wood and terra cotta were often used.  There are some pleasing aspects to all 
three projects, but because most of the buildings in the Historic District were built after 1900, and the 
majority of those in the 1920s-30s, the appropriateness of this mid-nineteenth century industrial building 
type for Downtown is subject to debate.  Notably, all three projects have discarded their building 
corners, something any respectable warehouse would never do. 
www.smheritage.org
Page  of 
6
8
Brooklyn Navy Yard, 
Brick Warehouses c 1860 - 1890s
31-57 S. B Street

SAN MATEO
HERITAGE ALLIANCE
June 6, 2023 
Muddlement 
Bespoke in the Downtown retail core, and Block 20 in the Downtown support/Gateway area, are both 
head scratchers.  Block 20 has an intriguing upper level, but it has no design connection to the lower 
floors. Why the upper level design doesn’t continue all the way to the street is vexing. The 3-story glass 
corner interrupts the continuity of the street wall, eliminating what would normally be a prominent visual 
anchor for the building. Firehouse Square in Belmont is a good example of what this project should be.  
Located one block from the Historic District, Bespoke appears to be a bundled collage of shapes, forms, 
wild ideas and conflicted energy.  One section resembles an open parking garage, another looks like an 
elongated version of Philip Johnson’s glass house perched atop two brick warehouses separated by a 3-
story glass divider.  Both proposals are so muddled it is impossible to guess what they are trying to do. 
 
www.smheritage.org

7
8
Bespoke
Block 20
Firehouse Square, Belmont

--- page 132 ---
SAN MATEO
HERITAGE ALLIANCE
June 6, 2023 
Missed Opportunity 
The only project within the Historic District, 180 E. Third Ave. makes a good faith effort to find 
compatibility.  It appears to be inspired by, and tries with insufficient guidance or direction, to follow the 
lead of the National Bank of San Mateo, a 1925 Greek Revival design by W. H. Weeks.  The new 
building, currently under construction, has pilasters, a classical entablature and some nice brick 
detailing.  But this is where the compatibility appears to end. The familiar symmetrical tripartite facade 
composition and prominent central entry that define this building type and style has been lost, and much 
of the compatibility along with it.   
Like all the other buildings proposed for Downtown, it is not one building but 
two - one anchored to the ground and the other, a different design and 
composition, setting awkwardly on top.  One wonders why this project did 
not draw its inspiration from the 5-story National Register eligible 1929 E.L. 
Norberg Medical Arts building diagonally across the street.  A missed 
opportunity, this project could have and should have been better. 
www.smheritage.org

180 E. Third Ave.
National Bank of San

CENTRAL NEIGHBORHOOD ASSOCIATION MEETING  
JUNE 21, 2023 
Post + Beam 
Harvest Properties & Prometheus  
Preston O’Connell -Partner, Harvest Properties 
Danielle Friend - Sr. Director Of Development- Harvest Properties 
Kevin Choy - Associate Director - Harvest Properties 
Jonathan Stone - Sr. Director- Prometheus  
Meeting comments on Post + Beam 
1. 
Parking concerns for residential tenants, especially those who own 2 cars.  There are only 8 
parking spaces and 59 residential units.  What measures will be taken to ensure that tenants will use 
The Metropolitan underground garage spaces?  
2. 
There was a concern about parking for the workers in the 253 commercial parking spaces.  It 
is not realistic to expect all office workers will take public transportation.  Will the office workers be 
able to park in The Metropolitan parking garage or is this only for the residential tenants?  
3. 
The design looks monolithic, industrial, and sterile.  This design resembles a warehouse or a 
factory.   A compatible design with our Historic District is important since this is one of the first 
buildings people will see as they come into the Downtown.   
4. 
The architectural style doesn’t fit the character of our neighborhood or Historic Downtown.  
The building needs more warmth and charm. 
5. 
The exterior designs with high flat walls could impact the pedestrian experience.  The flat 
walls on the Kiku Crossing housing project along 5th Avenue do impact the walking experience.   
The flat walls next to the sidewalk on this project are too high and are not pedestrian-friendly.  
6. 
You need to step the building down at a lower level and make the center of the building 
thinner and taller.  Harvest Properties and Prometheus are getting 2 free stories courtesy of the state, 
so they should easily be able to step back the design at the 3rd floor without completely sacrificing 
the plaza.  It might be smaller, but it can be done.  
  
7. 
Would like to see Contemporary Mediterranean architecture with arches, curved windows, 
and iron work, which would be more compatible with the neighborhood and Historic Downtown.   
8. 
How many people will be working in the office building?  This number will far exceed the 253 
parking spaces.  
9. 
Why are you building more office?  People are working remotely, and the office spaces are 
empty.   
10. 
How do we reach our RHNA numbers if you continue to build more office space?   
11. 
Are you going to do a traffic study of the cumulative traffic?  There are 22 new projects in 
various stages in the Downtown.  Are you going to do an EIR?

Downtown residents' life in an earthquake tragedy. This is just the fact. 
 
Our ancestors had defined some good earthquake safety measures. Our single family home has wooden walls because 
of earthquake danger. There are also setbacks even between same level single family homes. We really need to 
appreciate what our ancestors did to provide us an earthquake safe place to live in. Now, let's look at what we are doing 
to our future generations. Dense density kills. Setback saves life but we get rid of it. 
 
Dear commissioner, this is a life and death decision. Our kids' blood is in your hand. Please protect us. Please fight for 
our kids to provide an earthquake safe place.

RE: Central Neighborhood Association Meeting, June 21, 2023, Comments  
 
June 30th, 2023 
 
To: Central Neighborhood Association 
Cc: San Mateo Planning Commission and City Staff 
 
Dear Central Neighborhood Association,  
 
Thank you for your summation of the comments and questions that were stated at the Neighborhood 
Association Meeting on June 21st, 2023. We greatly appreciate Laurie Watanuki for opening up her 
home to the Neighborhood Association and our team to allow dialogue on this project between us all. 
To ensure City Staff and Planning Commission have full visibility into the session we would like to include 
our responses, noted in green below, that were provided to the group at the session. Should you have 
any further questions based on our responses feel free to reach out to us. 
1. Parking concerns for residential tenants, especially those who own 2 cars. There are only 8 
parking spaces and 59 residential units.  What measures will be taken to ensure that tenants will 
use The Metropolitan underground garage spaces? 
Answer: The 8 parking spaces on the ground floor of the residential building will be utilized for 
prospective tenants and guests. For residents with cars, part of their lease agreement can 
include a secured and guaranteed parking space below the Metropolitan Apartments, which 
currently has around 70 unused parking stalls in the block adjacent to our project. Our 
experience has been that residents prefer the secured and reserved parking spot in lieu of time 
limited street parking. 
2. There was a concern about parking for the workers in the 253 commercial parking spaces.  It is 
not realistic to expect all oﬃce workers will take public transportation.  Will the oﬃce workers 
be able to park in The Metropolitan parking garage or is this only for the residential tenants?  
Answer: The Harvest the Prometheus teams will complete a Traffic Demand Management and 
Transportation Impact Analysis study as part of the entitlement process to understand what 
issues exist regarding commercial parking if any. 
3. The design looks monolithic, industrial, and sterile.  This design resembles a warehouse or a 
factory.   A compatible design with our Historic District is important since this is one of the ﬁrst 
buildings people will see as they come into the Downtown.   
Answer: Thank you for the comment. The project is in its beginning stages of the entitlement 
process. We had originally targeted this particular design based on the project occurring within

--- page 141 ---
Page 2 of 3 
 
the transitionary zone between residential and commercial uses plus the sites historic industrial 
use. 
4. The architectural style doesn’t ﬁt the character of our neighborhood or Historic Downtown.  The 
building needs more warmth and charm. 
Answer: See answer to number 3. 
5. The exterior designs with high ﬂat walls could impact the pedestrian experience.  The ﬂat walls 
on the Kiku Crossing housing project along 5th Avenue do impact the walking experience.  The 
ﬂat walls next to the sidewalk on this project are too high and are not pedestrian-friendly. 
Answer: See answer to number 3.  
6. You need to step the building down at a lower level and make the center of the building thinner 
and taller.  Harvest Properties and Prometheus are getting 2 free stories courtesy of the state, 
so they should easily be able to step back the design at the 3rd ﬂoor without completely 
sacriﬁcing the plaza.  It might be smaller, but it can be done.  
Answer: This project prioritizes pedestrian experience based on what we have heard in San 
Mateo’s goals for the future. This includes increased sidewalk widths such that the public has 
the ability to enjoy well-designed, outdoor areas around our project. This requires that our 
project step back and build to certain heights that are greater than if we did not provide these 
publicly accessible spaces. The team is listening and will continue to evaluate these tradeoffs to 
support San Mateo. 
7. Would like to see Contemporary Mediterranean architecture with arches, curved windows, and 
iron work, which would be more compatible with the neighborhood and Historic Downtown.   
Answer: See answer to number 3.  
8. How many people will be working in the oﬃce building?  This number will far exceed the 253 
parking spaces.  
Answer:  The number of employees will exceed the number of parking spaces which is typical for 
office development today in locations close to transit. The team anticipates approximately 450 
to 525 employees in this office building, and as part of the entitlement process the team will 
undergo a Traffic Impact Analysis that will identify the approximate number of individuals who 
will commute to work via modes of transit other than single occupancy vehicles inclusive of but 
not limited to: Caltrain, SamTrans, biking, walking, and carpooling.

--- page 142 ---
Page 3 of 3 
 
9. Why are you building more oﬃce?  People are working remotely, and the oﬃce spaces are 
empty. 
Answer: Per a recent study by JLL, 60% of all office vacancy is housed within 10% of the 
structures and 1% of office buildings comprise 17% of office vacancy. Those buildings are older 
product that don’t meet the current demands for new tenants. That said, new construction is in 
demand more so than ever and will provide needed vibrancy in downtown San Mateo via 
supplying greater daytime population for the retail core. 
10. How do we reach our RHNA numbers if you continue to build more oﬃce space? 
Answer: San Mateo’s general plan contemplates additional office in concert with additional 
residential to ensure a balanced growth of San Mateo. This particular project location, well-
located and close to transit and amenities, is the ideal location for commercial office. That said, 
The Harvest and Prometheus team’s believe in residential development evidenced by our 
previous projects inclusive of Campus Drive, and have included 59 residential units in this 
development. 
11. Are you going to do a traﬃc study of the cumulative traﬃc?  There are 22 new projects in 
various stages in the Downtown.  Are you going to do an EIR? 
Answer: The project will meet all planning requirements for a property like ours inclusive of 
traffic impact studies. The General Plan’s EIR contemplated all traffic in the area based on the 
allotted densities and thus our project will study whether or not our impacts are anticipated to 
be greater than, equal to, or less than what was studied. 
   
Sincerely,  
 
Preston O’Connell, Kevin Choy, & Jonathan Stone 
Harvest Properties, Prometheus Real Estate Group

Dear San Mateo Planning Commission,
YIMBY Law is a 501(c)3 non-profit corporation, whose mission is to increase the accessibility
and affordability of housing in California. YIMBY Law sues municipalities when they fail to
comply with state housing laws, including the Housing Accountability Act (HAA). As you know,
the Planning Commission has an obligation to abide by all relevant state housing laws when
evaluating the above captioned proposal, including the HAA. Should the City fail to follow the
law, YIMBY Law will not hesitate to file suit to ensure that the law is enforced.
This project, located at 668 E 3rd Avenue & 316 S El Dorado Street, is a mixed-use project
inclusive of 59 residential units and 132,000 square feet of commercial office. The project
utilizes State Density Bonus to exceed the 3.0 zoned FAR in addition to building height plus
other limiting planning codes.
California Government Code § 65589.5, the Housing Accountability Act, prohibits localities
from denying housing development projects that are compliant with the locality’s zoning
ordinance or general plan at the time the application was deemed complete, unless the locality
can make findings that the proposed housing development would be a threat to public health
and safety. With the requested concessions and/or waivers available under State Density Bonus
Law, this project is zoning and general plan compliant.
The above captioned proposal is zoning compliant and general plan compliant, therefore, your
local agency must approve the application, or else make findings to the effect that the
proposed project would have an adverse impact on public health and safety, as described
above. Should the City fail to comply with the law, YIMBY Law will not hesitate to take legal
action to ensure that the law is enforced.
I am signing this letter both in my capacity as the Executive Director of YIMBY Law, and as a
resident of California who is affected by the shortage of housing in our state.
Sincerely,

600-680 E. 3RD AVE AND 316 S. ELDORADO ST.
SAN MATEO, CA 94401

SITE PLAN AND ARCHITECTURAL REVIEW (SPAR) – FINDINGS (SMMC 27.08.030): 
The application shall be approved if the City Council finds all of the following to exist:
1) The structures, site plan, and landscaping are in scale and harmonious with the character of the 
neighborhood;
2) The development will not be detrimental to the harmonious and orderly growth of the City; 
3) The development will not impair the desirability of investment or occupation in the vicinity, and 
otherwise is in the best interests of the public health, safety, or welfare;
4) The development meets all applicable standards as adopted by the Planning Commission and 
City Council, conforms with the General Plan, and will correct any violations of the zoning 
ordinance, building code, or other municipal codes that exist on the site;
5) The development will not adversely affect matters regarding police protection, crime 
prevention, and security.
SITE DEVELOPMENT PLANNING APPLICATION (SDPA) FOR GRADING AND TREE REMOVAL – FINDINGS 
(SMMC 23.40.040):
The application shall be approved if the City Council finds all of the following to exist:
1) All concerns regarding surface grading, structure foundations, drainage, subsurface conditions, 
erosion, and landscaping have been addressed as follows:
2) The project will result in the removal of __ trees, __ of which are considered Heritage Trees. 
The removal of these trees is necessary to accommodate the development of the proposed 
project. The project’s arborist report states that __.
3) All concerns regarding tree removal and protection of remaining trees on the site have been 
addressed as conditions of approval requiring conformance to the City's landscape regulations, 
through the provision of extensive on-site landscaping as shown on the project plans, and/or 
through the payment of a fee to the City’s tree planting fund.

RE: Support for 668 E 3rd Avenue & 316 S El Dorado Street
Dear San Mateo Planning Commission:
YIMBY Action is pleased to support the proposed project at 668 E 3rd Avenue & 316 S
El Dorado Street. This project would encompass a mixed-use project inclusive of 59
residential units and 132,000 square feet of commercial office. This project will help
address our citywide housing shortage and in particular, our need for more homes in
cities like San Mateo. Our support is especially relevant given this project’s close
proximity to nearby retail storefronts, existing multifamily housing units, and
Gateway Park.
YIMBY Action is a network of pro-housing activists fighting for more inclusive housing
policies. Our vision is an integrated society where every person has access to a safe,
affordable home near jobs, services, and opportunity.
1

--- page 155 ---
San Mateo’s severe housing shortage is causing skyrocketing homelessness and
poverty, crippling our economy, and leading to debilitating commutes that
exacerbate our global climate crisis. These impacts fall disproportionately on our
city’s low-income workers and families, and disproportionately deny communities of
color access to opportunity. If we strive to be a society that advances racial and class
justice, we must do more to ensure abundant housing in our region. This project will
help address the housing shortage and ensure a welcoming San Mateo where
everyone can thrive.
Best regards,
Laura Foote
YIMBY Action, Executive Director
YIMBY Action advocates for welcoming communities where
everyone can thrive.

Subject: Planning Commission Meeting, Tuesday, July 25 2023 Study Session#2 Post and Beam 
 
Good Evening to the Planning Commission.  
Tonight is very important to the Downtown Communities, North and Central neighborhoods to discuss 
the impact of the Harvest Properties, Post and Beam mixed-use project. 
 
This project will be two parcels, one an office building and the other, residential housing. It will have 253 
underground parking garage with the  entrance/exit onto South Eldorado Street. 
 
The project of these two buildings exceed the requirements of the current building codes of a 6 story 
building.   
 
The residential of 59 units, a mixture of studios, 1 and 2 bedroom units will add to the traffic on 4th 
Avenue. 
 
This parcel boarders along  between 3rd and 4th Avenues. These are the ways into Downtown San 
Mateo and out to Highway 101 going south, or to Shoreview. 
 
On the traffic corridors of both street corners, north-west and south-east there are only stop signs 
without Pedestrian crossings. 
 
This two way street is not even enough  room for parking on the street. It is too narrow even for the 
residents across the street to park.

--- page 158 ---
There are two items in this study session that requires the impact of street closures. These are the 
Water Treatment Plant and the Sanitary Sewer Connection. The Intersection  of South Delaware and 3rd 
Avenue will be completely unavailable for the flow of traffic for many months. 
 
It would be appreciated if we could wait for this project, Post and Beam, for two years. Then the Block 
21, PA-2023-15 project will only have 3rd and South Delaware Steets under construction  for a full year. 
 
Please remember all projects of these buildings construction need traffic mediation for Pedistrian safety. 
 
Thank you. 
 
Diana Pettit

From: Laurie Hietter 
  
Sent: Tuesday, July 25, 2023 3:43 PM 
To: Planning Commission <PlanningCommission@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Cc
 
Subject: Post & Beam Project 
 
Dear Planning Commission, 
 
Please consider the following points when discussing the Post & Beam proposal: 
1. This project will further exacerbate the jobs/housing imbalance. The project should include 
more housing and adequate parking. 
2. The project design is incompatible with historic San Mateo. We request the building include 
more classic design principles. The glass box style is jarring in juxtaposition with the historic 
central neighborhood, the historic downtown, and the Metropolitan housing development. Park 
Place (Bay Meadows phase 1) in San Mateo, Wheeler Plaza and Trestle Apartments in San Carlos 
are examples of compatible design.  
3. Please have the project design reviewed by a qualified architect who focuses on compatibility 
with historic locales. 
4. The project is too tall and will cause excessive shadow and create a wind tunnel. There should 
be detailed wind and shadow studies. 
5. The project construction and operation will cause significant traffic congestion. I am requesting 
that the City publish the cumulative traffic impact analysis including all planned, permitted, and 
proposed projects and require mitigation for the significant impacts in the project EIR.  
6. The project does not have adequate open space.  
7. Has the applicant provided any evidence of financial viability for this office project? 
8. The city has lost substantial sales tax and other revenue by having projects demolished and not 
built (Block 21, 3rd and El Camino, 37th and El Camino). 
Please consider these important issues.  
 
 
Thank you,

--- page 194 ---
Post+Beam Neighborhood Outreach
We have hosted two neighborhood meetings and met with multiple community members regarding our Post+Beam project.
What have we heard:
How are we responding to it:
Project does not relate to the 
neighborhood architecture and historic 
nature. 
Project team is reviewing the neighborhood architecture and updating the 
project architecture in the Major Application
Does the project provide enough parking 
for the proposed use?
The project provides appropriate parking for the commercial office project 
given its adjacency to Caltrain and public transit. The residences of the 
project will have access to the parking at the Metropolitan apartments 
allowing for 1:1 parking
The proposed project does not include any 
Retail.
The team is investigating the inclusion of Retail in the project.
What pedestrian improvements will be 
included within the project?
The team has proposed the expansion and improvement of the public 
realm surrounding the project inclusive of well-lit public plaza spaces in 
addition to well-curated landscaping around all three sides of the project.
Will the project increase traffic in the 
surrounding area?
As part of the entitlement process the team will complete a Transportation 
Impact Analysis study to better understand if any impacts to traffic are 
expected. Additionally, the team is looking into traffic calming measures.

--- page 207 ---
Post + Beam – Jobs-Housing Balance*
•
San Mateo’s jobs-housing balance grew from 1.14 in 2002 to 1.48 in 2019, but dropped to 1.36 in 2020 – all within the targeted 
1.0-1.5 range noted by City Council, the GPU, and City Consultants
•
San Mateo falls in line with county average jobs-housing balance
•
Harvest Properties’ portfolio of active San Mateo projects has a jobs-housing ratio of 1.05
•
Includes over 85% two-bedroom and larger units which accommodate greater than average number of jobs
*Statistics based on the “Jobs-Housing Balance Analysis: City of San Mateo” by Economic & Planning Systems, Inc. dated August 30th, 2022

--- page 208 ---
Post + Beam – Jobs-Housing Balance*
•
Adopted Housing Element includes approximately 10,000 housing units and the update to the General Plan looks to add 
approximately 15,000 jobs and seeks to add housing beyond what was listed in the Housing Element
•
General Plan proposes to add ~15,000 new jobs – the best location for that is near transit and downtown cores
•
Jobs-Housing balance on a site by site basis is unobtainable in small projects
•
Our project has maximized the residential unit count. A jobs-housing balance would result in only 15,000 sf of office 
resulting in less than 1.0 FAR. Far below City’s goal and ability for developers to transact
*Statistics based on the “Jobs-Housing Balance Analysis: City of San Mateo” by Economic & Planning Systems, Inc. dated August 30th, 2022

--- page 209 ---
Why Office? – Flight to Quality
•
Nationally, assets built 2015 and after have greater than 100 million square feet of net absorption since COVID-19. All other years 
of delivery are have negative net absorption
•
Nationally, vacancy rates in assets built in 2015 or later are 20% lower than that of older assets
•
Flight to quality has led to historically low renewal rates
•
95% of relocation leases have been executed to an upgraded building class or within the same level

--- page 210 ---
Why Office? - Office Vacancy
•
1% of Office inventory holds 17% of its vacancy. 30% holds 90% of the vacancy
•
Product built in the 1980s, 1970s, and before 1950s are the worst performing office assets

--- page 213 ---
Sustainability Goals and Wellness Features 
How can we develop a healthy workplace?
Our goal is to design and deliver high-quality, sustainable buildings that enhance occupant 
wellbeing and minimize impacts to carbon emissions and climate change.
Sustainability Goals
•
Significant Reduction in Carbon Emissions 
through use of Mass Timber
•
Carbon Neutral Design 
•
LEED Zero and / or LEED GOLD
•
WELL-Ready Certification
•
Drought-resistant landscaping
Wellness Features
•
Ample Natural light
•
Connection to Nature
•
Use of Natural Materials
•
Outdoor work and lounge areas
Health-Focused Design
•
Touchless / Automated Door Operators
•
Anti-microbial finishes on high-touch surfaces
•
Touchless Elevators
•
Enhanced Indoor Air Quality

--- page 214 ---
▪Reduces carbon emissions : According to a study done by University of 
Washington comparing “a hybrid, mid-rise, cross-laminated timber (CLT) 
commercial building” to “a reinforced concrete building with similar 
functional characteristics”, the CLT building represented a “26.5% 
reduction in global warming potential.”
▪Roughly 11%  of global greenhouse gas emissions come from building 
materials and construction. That’s what mass timber aims to reduce. The 
full lifecycle impact of mass timber on carbon emissions includes 3 
carbon effects
▪Supply chain: carbon-neutral wood supply 
▪Carbon embedding: 1 cubic meter of CLT wood sequesters 
roughly one tonne (1.1 US tons) of CO2 for 50 to hundreds of 
years
▪Substituting mass timber for concrete and steel: 2 tons of 
CO2 are emitted in the manufacture of a ton of steel. All 
those embodied emissions are avoided when CLT is 
substituted.
▪Performs very well in fire and meets code for fire safety 
▪Allows buildings to be constructed faster, with lower labor costs and less 
waste
▪The performance of mass timber in earthquakes has been much tested and 
proven remarkably good 
▪Aesthetically appealing by allowing expanses of exposed wood as part of 
the unique architectural design 
The Advantages of Mass Timber (CLT)
